<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">2014 Thai coup d'état</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
</div>

</tr>
<tr>
<td colspan="2" style="background-color:#DCDCDC;text-align:center;vertical-align:middle;">Part of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2013%E2%80%9314_Thai_political_crisis" title="2013–14 Thai political crisis">2013–14 Thai political crisis</a></td>

</tr>
<tr>
<td style="width:50%;border-right:1px dotted #aaa;"><a href="/wiki/National_Council_for_Peace_and_Order" title="National Council for Peace and Order">National Council for Peace and Order</a></td>

<tr>
<th colspan="2" style="background-color:#B0C4DE;text-align:center;vertical-align:middle;font-size:110%;">Commanders and leaders</th>
</tr>
<tr>
<td style="width:50%;border-right:1px dotted #aaa;"><a href="/wiki/Prayut_Chan-o-cha" title="Prayut Chan-o-cha">Prayut Chan-o-cha</a></td>
<td style="width:50%;padding-left:0.25em"><a href="/wiki/Niwatthamrong_Boonsongpaisan" title="Niwatthamrong Boonsongpaisan">Niwatthamrong Boonsongpaisan</a></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>On 22 May 2014, the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Thai_Armed_Forces" title="Royal Thai Armed Forces">Royal Thai Armed Forces</a>, led by General <a href="/wiki/Prayut_Chan-o-cha" title="Prayut Chan-o-cha">Prayut Chan-o-cha</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_Commanders_of_the_Royal_Thai_Army" title="List of Commanders of the Royal Thai Army">Commander</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Thai_Army" title="Royal Thai Army">Royal Thai Army</a> (RTA), launched a <i><a href="/wiki/Coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="Coup d'état">coup d'état</a></i>, the 12th since <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thai_Revolution_of_1932" title="Thai Revolution of 1932">the country's first coup in 1932</a>, against the <a href="/wiki/Yingluck_cabinet" title="Yingluck cabinet">caretaker government</a> of <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a>, following six months of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2013%E2%80%9314_Thai_political_crisis" title="2013–14 Thai political crisis">political crisis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WaPo12coups_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-WaPo12coups-1">[1]</a></sup> The military established a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Military_junta" title="Military junta">junta</a> called the <a href="/wiki/National_Council_for_Peace_and_Order" title="National Council for Peace and Order">National Council for Peace and Order</a> (NCPO) to govern the nation.</p>
<p>After dissolving the government and the <a href="/wiki/Senate_of_Thailand" title="Senate of Thailand">Senate</a>, the NCPO vested executive and legislative powers in its leader and ordered the judicial branch to operate under its directives. In addition, it partially repealed the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2007_Constitution_of_Thailand" title="2007 Constitution of Thailand">2007 constitution</a>, save the second chapter which concerns the king,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> declared martial law and curfew nationwide, banned political gatherings, arrested and detained <a href="/wiki/Politician" title="Politician">politicians</a> and anti-coup activists, imposed <a href="/wiki/Internet_censorship_in_Thailand" title="Internet censorship in Thailand">internet censorship</a> and took control of the media.</p>
<p>The NCPO issued an <a href="/wiki/2014_interim_constitution_of_Thailand" title="2014 interim constitution of Thailand">interim constitution</a> granting itself amnesty and sweeping power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> The NCPO also established a military dominated <a href="/wiki/National_Assembly_of_Thailand" title="National Assembly of Thailand">national legislature</a> which later unanimously elected General Prayut as the new <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_Thailand" title="Prime Minister of Thailand">prime minister</a> of the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2014_Thai_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2013%E2%80%9314_Thai_political_crisis" title="2013–14 Thai political crisis">2013–14 Thai political crisis</a></div>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Thai_general_election,_2011" title="Thai general election, 2011">2011 general election</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yingluck_Shinawatra" title="Yingluck Shinawatra">Yingluck Shinawatra</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Pheu_Thai_Party" title="Pheu Thai Party">Pheu Thai Party</a> (PTP) won a landslide victory and formed the government with Yingluck as prime minister. Anti-government protests, led by former <a href="/wiki/Democrat_Party_(Thailand)" title="Democrat Party (Thailand)">Democrat Party</a> secretary general <a href="/wiki/Suthep_Thaugsuban" title="Suthep Thaugsuban">Suthep Thaugsuban</a>, began in November 2013. Suthep later formed the <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Democratic_Reform_Committee" title="People's Democratic Reform Committee">People's Democratic Reform Committee</a> (PDRC) for the purpose of demanding the establishment of an unelected "people's council" to supervise "political reform". Pro-government groups, including the <a href="/wiki/United_Front_for_Democracy_Against_Dictatorship" title="United Front for Democracy Against Dictatorship">Red Shirts</a>, held mass rallies in response. Violence occasionally occurred, resulting in a number of deaths and injuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>In December 2013, Yingluck dissolved the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/House_of_Representatives_of_Thailand" title="House of Representatives of Thailand">House of Representatives</a> and scheduled a general election for 2 February 2014. Disrupted by the anti-government protesters, the election was not completed on that day. The <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_Court_of_Thailand" title="Constitutional Court of Thailand">Constitutional Court</a> then nullified the election on 21 March 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> On 7 May 2014, the Constitutional Court unanimously removed Yingluck and nine other senior ministers from office over the controversial transfer of a top security officer in 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ylremoval_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-ylremoval-9">[9]</a></sup> The remaining ministers selected Deputy Prime Minister and Minister of Commerce <a href="/wiki/Niwatthamrong_Boonsongpaisan" title="Niwatthamrong Boonsongpaisan">Niwatthamrong Boonsongpaisan</a> to replace Yingluck as caretaker prime minister as protests continued.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Immediately_prior_to_the_coup">Immediately prior to the coup</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2014_Thai_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Immediately prior to the coup">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Martial_law_imposition">Martial law imposition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2014_Thai_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Martial law imposition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Prayuth_Jan-ocha_2010-06-17_Cropped.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2596" data-file-width="2220" height="257" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Prayuth_Jan-ocha_2010-06-17_Cropped.jpg/220px-Prayuth_Jan-ocha_2010-06-17_Cropped.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Prayuth_Jan-ocha_2010-06-17_Cropped.jpg/330px-Prayuth_Jan-ocha_2010-06-17_Cropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Prayuth_Jan-ocha_2010-06-17_Cropped.jpg/440px-Prayuth_Jan-ocha_2010-06-17_Cropped.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Prayuth_Jan-ocha_2010-06-17_Cropped.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
General <a href="/wiki/Prayut_Chan-o-cha" title="Prayut Chan-o-cha">Prayut Chan-o-cha</a>, RTA Commander.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The RTA intervened on 20 May 2014, when its commander, General Prayut, exercised the power under the act promulgated by King <a href="/wiki/Vajiravudh#Administration.2C_Economy.2C_and_Infrastructure" title="Vajiravudh">Vajiravudh</a> (<a href="/wiki/Rama_(Kings_of_Thailand)" title="Rama (Kings of Thailand)">Rama</a> VI) entitled <i>Martial Law, BE 2457 (1914)</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vajiravudh_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-vajiravudh-11">[11]</a></sup> to impose <a href="/wiki/Martial_law#Thailand" title="Martial law">martial law</a> nationwide from 03:00. He said through a television pool that the imposition of martial law was due to continuous violence from both sides and was for the purpose of allowing the army to restore and maintain the peace.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>After imposing martial law, General Prayut ordered the dissolution of the Centre for Administration of Peace and Order, a police body formed by the caretaker government to resolve the crisis. He then established a Peace and Order Maintaining Command (POMC) with himself as its commander. Charged with the duty to "restore peace to the people from all sides without delay", the POMC was initially given the power to "prevent, suppress, abate and resolve" any situation affecting national security, to enforce every provision of the martial law, and to summon any person. General Prayut also ordered the personnel of the police, navy, air force and <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Defence_(Thailand)" title="Ministry of Defence (Thailand)">Ministry of Defence</a> to be subject to the POMC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-capodissolved_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-capodissolved-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Bangkok" title="Bangkok">Bangkok</a>, soldiers were stationed in many places<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> and the main roads were blockaded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> The military retook the Government House from the PDRC protesters<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> and seized all television stations in Bangkok and some other parts of the country<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> before shutting down some stations, including those of the PDRC and the Red Shirts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> General Prayut later ordered all media to replace their regular programs with the POMC programs whenever he required.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> and imposed a ban on publishing information which might affect the military's mission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> He also directed all government agency chiefs to report to him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 21 May 2014, the POMC established an internet censorship task force and ordered <a href="/wiki/Internet_service_provider" title="Internet service provider">internet service providers</a> to report to it in order to control online content.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<p>The caretaker government complained that it had not been consulted about the martial law-related decisions of the army. The army claimed that the move was not a coup d'état and that the government remained in office.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Unsuccessful_talks">Unsuccessful talks</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2014_Thai_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Unsuccessful talks">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>After its establishment, the POMC held talks with rival groups for two days in an attempt to find resolution. It was reported that the participants were of conflicting opinions and no agreement could be adopted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NCPO_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-NCPO-27">[27]</a></sup> During talks, the caretaker government was requested by the <a href="/wiki/Election_Commission_of_Thailand" title="Election Commission of Thailand">Election Commission</a> to resign.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> The government denied the request, saying it was required by the constitution to stay in office.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-flee_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-flee-29">[29]</a></sup> The 2007 constitution prescribed that the cabinet vacates office en bloc upon dissolution of the House of Representatives but it shall remain in office and continue to function until a new cabinet takes office.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<p>On the afternoon of 22 May 2014, the POMC held another talk at the Thai Army Club on <a href="/wiki/Vibhavadi_Rangsit_Road" title="Vibhavadi Rangsit Road">Vibhavadi Rangsit Road</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NCPO_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-NCPO-27">[27]</a></sup> The talk had proceeded for five hours<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> without reaching agreement, whilst the government maintained that it bore a legal duty to remain in office. At the meeting, General Prayut eventually said to Minister of Justice Chaikasem Nitisiri who led the government representatives: "The talk knows no end because you all only speak about the law. [...] The government insists that it won't resign, right?" Chaikasem replied: "Not at this moment". General Prayut then told the meeting that "Sorry, I must seize power"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> and ordered the detention of the cabinet members as well as the leaders of the PDRC, Red Shirts, and political parties who attended the talk, much to the dismay of the media representatives who were present.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NCPO_27-2"><a href="#cite_note-NCPO-27">[27]</a></sup> They were held at the First Infantry Regiment of the King's Guards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Coup">Coup</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2014_Thai_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Coup">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2014_0526_Thailand_coup_Chang_Phueak_Gate_Chiang_Mai_01.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1741" data-file-width="2560" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/2014_0526_Thailand_coup_Chang_Phueak_Gate_Chiang_Mai_01.jpg/220px-2014_0526_Thailand_coup_Chang_Phueak_Gate_Chiang_Mai_01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/2014_0526_Thailand_coup_Chang_Phueak_Gate_Chiang_Mai_01.jpg/330px-2014_0526_Thailand_coup_Chang_Phueak_Gate_Chiang_Mai_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/2014_0526_Thailand_coup_Chang_Phueak_Gate_Chiang_Mai_01.jpg/440px-2014_0526_Thailand_coup_Chang_Phueak_Gate_Chiang_Mai_01.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2014_0526_Thailand_coup_Chang_Phueak_Gate_Chiang_Mai_01.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Soldiers at Chang Phueak Gate, <a href="/wiki/Chiang_Mai" title="Chiang Mai">Chiang Mai</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Launch">Launch</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2014_Thai_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Launch">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>On the evening of 22 May 2014, General Prayut announced through a televised address that the armed forces were assuming control of national administration,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> formally launching a coup d'état against the caretaker government and establishing the NCPO to rule the state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NCPO_27-3"><a href="#cite_note-NCPO-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<p>Later that night, the NCPO repealed the 2007 constitution, save the second chapter which deals with the <a href="/wiki/Monarchy_of_Thailand" title="Monarchy of Thailand">king</a>. In addition, it formally ordered the dissolution of the caretaker government, but the Senate as well as all other state agencies, including the courts and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Independent_agencies_of_the_Thai_government" title="Independent agencies of the Thai government">independent organs</a>, were kept intact.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> The NCPO declared its composition as follows:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-disperse_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-disperse-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>General Prayut (RTA Commander) as its leader,</li>
<li>General Thanasak Patimaprakorn (<a href="/wiki/List_of_Commanders_of_the_Royal_Thai_Armed_Forces_Headquarters" title="List of Commanders of the Royal Thai Armed Forces Headquarters">Chief of Defence Forces</a>), Admiral Narong Pipathanasai (<a href="/wiki/Royal_Thai_Navy" title="Royal Thai Navy">Royal Thai Navy</a> Commander), Air Chief Marshal Prajin Jantong (<a href="/wiki/Royal_Thai_Air_Force" title="Royal Thai Air Force">Royal Thai Air Force</a> Commander), and Police General Adul Saengsingkaew (<a href="/wiki/Royal_Thai_Police" title="Royal Thai Police">Royal Thai Police</a> Commissioner General), as its deputy leaders,</li>
<li>General Udomdet Sitabut (RTA Deputy Commander) as its secretary general.</li>
</ul>
<p>The NCPO then announced that its leader would exercise all powers and duties which the laws invest in the prime minister and the cabinet, until there was a new prime minister.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> It placed its members in charge of government ministries and equivalent agencies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shortlong_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-shortlong-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 24 May 2014, the NCPO dissolved the existing Senate and granted legislative powers to its leader.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sndissolved_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-sndissolved-42">[42]</a></sup> It ordered the judicial branch to operate under its directives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> It transferred Police General Adul to an inactive post in the <a href="/wiki/Office_of_the_Prime_Minister_(Thailand)" title="Office of the Prime Minister (Thailand)">Office of the Prime Minister</a> and replaced him with Police General Watcharapol Prasarnrajkit. Adul was seen as a loyalist of the deposed government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sndissolved_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-sndissolved-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Arrest_and_detention_of_public_figures">Arrest and detention of public figures</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2014_Thai_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Arrest and detention of public figures">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Immediately after the coup was launched, caretaker Prime Minister Niwatthamrong, who did not attend the talks, left his office at the <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Commerce_(Thailand)" title="Ministry of Commerce (Thailand)">Ministry of Commerce</a> so as to avoid being apprehended by the military.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-flee_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-flee-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> The NCPO ordered him and the cabinet members who were not detained to report to the military that day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> It was reported that Niwatthamrong attempted to establish a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Government-in-exile" title="Government-in-exile">government-in-exile</a> at the United States embassy in Bangkok, but the embassy denied the report.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>On the night of 22 May 2014, military officers detained additional politicians, including <a href="/wiki/Chalerm_Yubamrung" title="Chalerm Yubamrung">Chalerm Yubamrung</a> and his sons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> The following day, the NCPO summoned PTP members and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shinawatra" title="Shinawatra">Shinawatra family</a> members, including Yingluck.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> Niwatthamrong and Yingluck surrendered themselves to the NCPO on that day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> Yingluck was detained at an undisclosed "safe house".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-former_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-former-52">[52]</a></sup> The NPOC said it released her on 25 May.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<p>The NCPO later summoned another 114 prominent figures from both sides<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> and stated that those who refused to submit to it would be apprehended and prosecuted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> Activist <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Sombat_Boonngamanong&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Sombat Boonngamanong (page does not exist)">Sombat Boonngamanong</a>, better known as Dotty Editor, was the first person who refused to surrender, saying "Hilarious. Not reporting [to the junta] is considered a criminal offense." He challenged the summons with a post on Facebook: "Catch me if you can".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> The NCPO responded by stating that on the morning of 24 May, it would send soldiers to apprehend those who failed to appear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> One of those apprehended on that day was <a href="/wiki/Panthongtae_Shinawatra" title="Panthongtae Shinawatra">Panthongtae Shinawatra</a>, a nephew of Yingluck.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> Sombat was eventually apprehended on 5 June 2014 in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chon_Buri_Province" title="Chon Buri Province">Chon Buri Province</a>. The military tracked him down through the internet, using the IP address he used to post his comments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taunting_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-taunting-60">[60]</a></sup> In addition to two years in jail for violating its orders, the military said Sombat will face seven years of imprisonment on grounds of encouraging people to violate its orders, which are the "law of the nation". The military also said that those who provided refuge to Sombat were to be dealt with by two years in jail on account of "harbouring a criminal".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<p>The NCPO also summoned the Bangkok diplomatic corps to its headquarters. In its summons, it said the corps were merely "invited" to a meeting with the military figures, so that they would have "correct understanding" of its mission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> Many foreign ambassadors, including German Ambassador Rolf Peter Schulze, refused to go.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup></p>
<p>Former Minister of Education <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chaturon_Chaisaeng" title="Chaturon Chaisaeng">Chaturon Chaisaeng</a> was the first person to be tried in a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Military_court" title="Military court">military court</a> for failing to appear as summoned by the NCPO. Soldiers apprehended Chaturon in front of a crowd of foreign journalists immediately after giving a press conference at the Foreign Correspondents' Club of Thailand on 27 May 2014. An NCPO spokesman said that holding a press conference with foreign media was deemed improper and against NCPO policy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> Chaturon faced fourteen years of imprisonment in consequence of computer crime charges.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
<p>Martial law allows the military to detain people for no more than seven days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vajiravudh_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-vajiravudh-11">[11]</a></sup> The NCPO did not observe the time limit. Some individuals were <a href="/wiki/Solitary_confinement" title="Solitary confinement">held incommunicado</a> for more than three weeks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Control_of_public_activities_and_media">Control of public activities and media</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2014_Thai_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Control of public activities and media">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Upon announcing the coup, the NCPO laid down a ban on political gatherings and directed all protesters to disperse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-disperse_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-disperse-37">[37]</a></sup> It ordered all educational institutes, both public and private, to close from 23–25 May 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> It imposed a curfew throughout the country, ordering people to stay indoors from 22:00 to 05:00.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> The curfew time was later changed to 24:00 to 04:00 hours, effective 28 May 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> The curfew was lifted in most parts of the country on 13 June 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup></p>
<p>The NCPO ordered all television and radio stations to stop airing their regular programs and to broadcast RTA programs only.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> It detained Wanchai Tantiwittayapitak, deputy director of the <a href="/wiki/Thai_Public_Broadcasting_Service" title="Thai Public Broadcasting Service">Thai Public Broadcasting Service</a> which operates Thai PBS station, after he permitted the station to live broadcast a special program about the coup on YouTube instead of television.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> On the program, several scholars, including <a href="/wiki/Chulalongkorn_University" title="Chulalongkorn University">Chulalongkorn University</a> lecturer Gothom Arya, were interviewed and gave negative comments on the coup.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> Thai PBS said Wanchai was taken to the First Army Area headquarters to "tune in understanding between the media and army".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 23 May 2014, the NCPO summoned all media chiefs to the Thai Army Club and ordered internet service providers to censor any information deemed provocative, causing public disturbance, containing official secrets, likely detrimental to national security, or defamatory to the NCPO.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> It threatened to shut down social media if its operator failed to block information inciting unrest or inciting "opposition to peacekeeping".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<p>On the afternoon of 23 May, analogue television networks, except Thai PBS, were allowed to resume their normal programming,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> after the NCPO ordered the internet service providers to block all broadcast sharing attempts on the internet and instructed the National Broadcasting and Telecommunications Commission to shut down internet-based television.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> All digital television networks were permitted to resume broadcasts the following day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 24 May 2014, media organisations issued an open letter urging the NCPO to end the restriction of press freedom as soon as possible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> The NCPO responded by summoning all media operators, instructing them that they were required to attend a meeting with the NCPO first.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-coup">Post-coup</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2014_Thai_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Post-coup">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Future_roles">Future roles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2014_Thai_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Future roles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The NCPO stated on 23 May 2014 that it aimed to conduct national "economic, social and political" reforms before elections were permitted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-whattodo_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-whattodo-85">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> It said a reform council and a national assembly will be set up to work on the reforms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-reform_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-reform-87">[87]</a></sup> It also said it wanted to handle, inter alia, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2013%E2%80%9314_Thai_political_crisis#Rice-pledge_scheme_crisis" title="2013–14 Thai political crisis">rice pledge project</a> problems, and will carry out a project of constructing roads on both banks of the <a href="/wiki/Chao_Phraya_River" title="Chao Phraya River">Chao Phraya River</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-whattodo_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-whattodo-85">[85]</a></sup> The road construction project was originally initiated by the PTP in 2012 and was part of a flood control project.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup></p>
<p>General Prayut said he would run the country until the situation required an interim government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-reform_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-reform-87">[87]</a></sup> In administering the country, he said he will use the <a href="/wiki/Command_and_control" title="Command and control">military command and control systems</a> which he believed would develop the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-comprehensivereform_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-comprehensivereform-89">[89]</a></sup> The NCPO announced that its leader would determine national administrative policies, both "short-term and long-term".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shortlong_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-shortlong-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<p>Unlike previous coups, there were no promises of a quick return to civilian rule.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-former_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-former-52">[52]</a></sup> On 26 May, the NCPO held a press conference at which a <i><a href="/wiki/Thai_Rath" title="Thai Rath">Thai Rath</a></i> journalist asked General Prayut if he would become the new prime minister himself. Prayut did not refuse, answering, "It is already in the plan." Another journalist, from the <i><a href="/wiki/Bangkok_Post" title="Bangkok Post">Bangkok Post</a></i>, asked when an election would happen. Prayut replied, "[An election] depends on the situation. [There is] no deadline. That's enough", before walking out of the conference room.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> The following day, both journalists were summoned by the NCPO on grounds of "giving questions that lessen public confidence in the ruler".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup></p>
<p>An NCPO spokesman also said that normal democratic principles could not be applied at the current time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Sydney_Morning_Herald" title="The Sydney Morning Herald">The Sydney Morning Herald</a></i> reported that the military's decisions showed that General Prayut is preparing "a long-term pervasive takeover without the calling of quick elections".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 29 May 2014, Lieutenant General Chatchalerm Chalermsukh, deputy army chief of staff, told reporters at a press conference in Bangkok, that while elections would be held, there was no timetable. "Right conditions" would have to be put in place before an election took place, he said. A day later, in a national television address, General Prayut said elections would not be held for at least fifteen months. He warned against resistance to the military and called on all sides to co-operate and stop protesting.</p>
<p>Some observers believe that elections are being postponed due to issues of succession to the throne of Thailand. The king's "...death will shake Thailand like nothing has in its modern history, and the Thai military wants to be firmly in charge when that happens. It is that simple."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Royal_endorsement">Royal endorsement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2014_Thai_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Royal endorsement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="mbox-small plainlinks sistersitebox" role="presentation" style="border:1px solid #aaa;background-color:#f9f9f9">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wikisource-logo.svg"><img alt="" class="noviewer" data-file-height="430" data-file-width="410" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/38px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/57px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/76px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png 2x" width="38"/></a></td>
<td class="mbox-text plainlist"><a href="/wiki/Wikisource" title="Wikisource">Wikisource</a> has original text related to this article:
<div style="margin-left: 10px;"><b><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Proclamation_on_Appointment_of_Leader_of_the_National_Council_for_Peace_and_Order_dated_24_May_2014" title="wikisource:Proclamation on Appointment of Leader of the National Council for Peace and Order dated 24 May 2014">Proclamation on Appointment of Leader of the National Council for Peace and Order</a></b></div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>On 24 May 2014, the NCPO said King <a href="/wiki/Bhumibol_Adulyadej" title="Bhumibol Adulyadej">Bhumibol Adulyadej</a> had acknowledged the coup, but stopped short of describing the response as an endorsement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> However, on 26 May 2014 the king formally appointed General Prayut to run the country. In Thailand the monarchy is highly respected and royal endorsement was seen as legitimation of the takeover.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="L.C3.A8se_majest.C3.A9_cases">Lèse majesté cases</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2014_Thai_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Lèse majesté cases">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>At a press conference on 23 May 2014, the NCPO said one of its missions is to "severely" deal with <i><a href="/wiki/L%C3%A8se_majest%C3%A9_in_Thailand" title="Lèse majesté in Thailand">lèse majesté</a></i> offenders in Thailand and ordered governmental officers to support this mission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-comprehensivereform_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-comprehensivereform-89">[89]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 25 May 2014, the NCPO authorised military courts to try all cases concerning <i>lèse majesté</i>, sedition, national security, and violation of NCPO orders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup></p>
<p>On that day, the NCPO searched the residence of <a href="/wiki/Somyot_Prueksakasemsuk" title="Somyot Prueksakasemsuk">Somyot Prueksakasemsuk</a>, an imprisoned magazine editor sentenced in 2013 to 11 years in jail for <i>lèse majesté</i>. The NCPO detained his wife, who had been campaigning for <a href="/wiki/Political_prisoner" title="Political prisoner">political prisoners</a>, and his son, a fourth year student at the <a href="/wiki/Faculty_of_Law,_Thammasat_University" title="Faculty of Law, Thammasat University">Faculty of Law, Thammasat University</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> Other critics of the <i>lèse majesté</i> law were detained as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup></p>
<p>The NCPO later summoned a number of exiled activists accused of <i>lèse majesté</i>, including former Chulalongkorn University political science lecturer <a href="/wiki/Giles_Ji_Ungpakorn" title="Giles Ji Ungpakorn">Giles Ji Ungpakorn</a> and former minister <a href="/wiki/Jakrapob_Penkair" title="Jakrapob Penkair">Jakrapob Penkair</a>. They were ordered to appear by 9 June 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Interim_constitution">Interim constitution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2014_Thai_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Interim constitution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/2014_interim_constitution_of_Thailand" title="2014 interim constitution of Thailand">2014 interim constitution of Thailand</a></div>
<p>An <a href="/wiki/2014_interim_constitution_of_Thailand" title="2014 interim constitution of Thailand">interim constitution</a> was enacted on 22 July 2014, paving the way for the establishment of a national legislature, a provisional government, and a reform council.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> The draft interim constitution originally required that a draft new constitution be approved by the citizens in a nationwide referendum before it is forwarded to the king for his signature. The requirement was disapproved by the NCPO and was deleted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-draft_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-draft-102">[102]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Wissanu_Krea-ngam" title="Wissanu Krea-ngam">Wissanu Krea-ngam</a>, a Chulalongkorn University law lecturer who drafted the interim constitution, explained that the requirement was deleted to avoid "lengthy process".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wisanu_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-wisanu-103">[103]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Domestic_responses">Domestic responses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2014_Thai_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Domestic responses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pro-_and_anti-government_protesters">Pro- and anti-government protesters</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2014_Thai_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Pro- and anti-government protesters">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Many PDRC protesters lauded the announcement of the coup at their rally sites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> Phra Buddha Issara, a Buddhist monk and PDRC co-leader, went on stage to proclaim the victory of the anti-Shinawatra government protesters before requesting that his followers disperse and go home.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jubilant_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-jubilant-105">[105]</a></sup> Soldiers took over the pro-government protest sites and fired shots in the air to disperse protesters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup></p>
<p>The last group of protesters left Bangkok on the evening of 23 May 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> The NCPO provided 70 military vehicles to carry the protesters from both sides home.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Anti-coup">Anti-coup</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2014_Thai_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Anti-coup">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Public_protests">Public protests</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2014_Thai_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Public protests">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In spite of a ban on political gatherings of more than five persons,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> demonstrators, including <a href="/wiki/Thammasat_University" title="Thammasat University">Thammasat University</a> students, marched to the <a href="/wiki/Democracy_Monument" title="Democracy Monument">Democracy Monument</a> on 23 May 2014 to express their anger at the coup.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-anti-coup_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-anti-coup-110">[110]</a></sup> However the military stopped them from reaching the monument, citing fear of violence and clashes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup></p>
<p>On the same day, separate protests took place outside the <a href="/wiki/Bangkok_Art_and_Culture_Centre" title="Bangkok Art and Culture Centre">Bangkok Art and Culture Centre</a>, led by freelance media, artists, and social activists. Soldiers sent by the NCPO detained the protesters. A live round was fired and the whereabouts of those apprehended was not known.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 24 May 2014, a group held an anti-coup protest in front of Major Ratchayothin Cinema in Bangkok, where a company of troops with riot shields was stationed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> On that evening, another group of at least 1,000 protesters marched to the <a href="/wiki/Victory_Monument_(Thailand)" title="Victory Monument (Thailand)">Victory Monument</a>. The military tried to stop their procession, using a police truck. Some of the protesters were apprehended but were released after negotiation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup></p>
<p>At midday on 25 May 2014, a group of anti-coup protesters gathered in front of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/McDonalds" title="McDonalds">McDonalds</a> restaurant at <a href="/wiki/Ratchaprasong" title="Ratchaprasong">Ratchaprasong</a>, in the heart of Bangkok. Soldiers arrived and seized the area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> Another group of people tried to march from the Victory Monument to the <a href="/wiki/Pathum_Wan_District" title="Pathum Wan District">Pathum Wan intersection</a> protesting the coup. The military managed to block them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup></p>
<p>Anti-coup protests also took place outside Bangkok. In <a href="/wiki/Chiang_Mai_Province" title="Chiang Mai Province">Chiang Mai Province</a>, a group of people clad in black held anti-coup activities for days, including marching to the city's ancient walls where they performed a candle-lit ceremony opposing the military's action.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> Some of them were apprehended by military on 24 May.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> Anti-coup rallies also occurred in several other provinces. In <a href="/wiki/Khon_Kaen_Province" title="Khon Kaen Province">Khon Kaen Province</a>, students held a ceremony to bid farewell to democracy in front of Central Plaza Khon Kaen shopping mall where soldiers were stationed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Maha_Sarakham_Province" title="Maha Sarakham Province">Maha Sarakham Province</a>, students held an anti-coup protest in the middle of the city but soldiers came and seized protest materials such as banners.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> A group of Thais living in <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a> held anti-coup activities at <a href="/wiki/Kings_Park,_Western_Australia" title="Kings Park, Western Australia">Kings Park</a>, <a href="/wiki/Perth" title="Perth">Perth</a>, on 26 May.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup></p>
<p>A number of anti-coup activists outside Bangkok were later summoned by regional military courts and were detained without charges.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 10 June 2014, Chalard Vorachat, a retired <a href="/wiki/Pilot_officer" title="Pilot officer">pilot officer</a> and renowned <a href="/wiki/Hunger_strike" title="Hunger strike">hunger striker</a>, sued the NCPO before the <a href="/wiki/Criminal_Court_of_Thailand" title="Criminal Court of Thailand">Criminal Court</a>. Chalard alleged that the NCPO, by staging the coup, committed insurrection. The court dismissed the case, ruling that Chalard was not the victim and was therefore not entitled to institute the case, although Chalard claimed that he was the victim because his rights and liberties had been affected by the coup.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Online_activities">Online activities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2014_Thai_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Online activities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Thai-coup-detat-2014-social-media-banner.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1971" data-file-width="2606" height="303" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Thai-coup-detat-2014-social-media-banner.jpg/400px-Thai-coup-detat-2014-social-media-banner.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Thai-coup-detat-2014-social-media-banner.jpg/600px-Thai-coup-detat-2014-social-media-banner.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Thai-coup-detat-2014-social-media-banner.jpg/800px-Thai-coup-detat-2014-social-media-banner.jpg 2x" width="400"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Thai-coup-detat-2014-social-media-banner.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A banner in Bangkok, observed on 3 June 2014, informs the public that "like" or "share" activity on social media could land them in prison. The banner says this is "for the sake of the monarchy". near Chit Lom, Bangkok, Thailand on 2014-06-30. The woman in the picture on the right is Chatwadee Amornpat, known as 'London Rose'.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Thailand_Ministry_of_Information_and_Communication_Technology_2014_Censorship_Image.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="1280" height="250" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Thailand_Ministry_of_Information_and_Communication_Technology_2014_Censorship_Image.jpg/400px-Thailand_Ministry_of_Information_and_Communication_Technology_2014_Censorship_Image.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Thailand_Ministry_of_Information_and_Communication_Technology_2014_Censorship_Image.jpg/600px-Thailand_Ministry_of_Information_and_Communication_Technology_2014_Censorship_Image.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Thailand_Ministry_of_Information_and_Communication_Technology_2014_Censorship_Image.jpg/800px-Thailand_Ministry_of_Information_and_Communication_Technology_2014_Censorship_Image.jpg 2x" width="400"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Thailand_Ministry_of_Information_and_Communication_Technology_2014_Censorship_Image.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Image displayed from Thailand's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Information_and_Communication_Technology_(Thailand)" title="Ministry of Information and Communication Technology (Thailand)">Ministry of Information and Communication Technology</a> when accessing prohibited content, such as <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Daily_Mail" title="The Daily Mail">The Daily Mail</a></i>, from Thailand in 2014.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Anti-coup sentiment was also echoed on Thai social media such as Facebook, Twitter, and LINE,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-anti-coup_110-1"><a href="#cite_note-anti-coup-110">[110]</a></sup> even though the military had previously warned that it would block social media if material critical of the coup was published.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup></p>
<p>A reporter, Pornthip Mongyai, was dismissed by her news agency, Mono Group, after a photo of Pornthip, wearing a press armband, stood next to a line of soldiers with an "X" taped over her lips, went viral on the internet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup></p>
<p>In response to anti-coup activities on social media, the NCPO ordered the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Information_and_Communication_Technology_(Thailand)" title="Ministry of Information and Communication Technology (Thailand)">Ministry of Information and Communication Technology</a> (MICT) to block Facebook in Thailand "from time to time", effective from 28 May 2014. On that evening, Facebook was blocked for about an hour throughout the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> The MICT stated on 24 May that more than 100 URLs have been blocked under martial law.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup></p>
<p>People calling for protests on social media were warned that they will face prosecution for sedition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> The military also said that <a href="/wiki/Like_button" title="Like button">liking</a> an anti-coup page on Facebook constituted a criminal offence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Anti-coup_symbols">Anti-coup symbols</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2014_Thai_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Anti-coup symbols">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Thai coup protesters used the three-finger salute from <a href="/wiki/The_Hunger_Games_(film_series)" title="The Hunger Games (film series)"><i>The Hunger Games</i> film series</a>, symbolising their opposition to the coup.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> Three extended fingers represent equality, liberty, and brotherhood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> The military announced that it would arrest anyone who displayed the salute.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hunger_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-hunger-135">[135]</a></sup></p>
<p>Protesters then adopted the <a href="/wiki/Sandwich" title="Sandwich">sandwich</a> as their new anti-coup symbol. They handed out sandwiches, shouting "sandwiches for democracy!".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> On 22 June 2014, a student eating sandwiches in front of <a href="/wiki/Siam_Paragon" title="Siam Paragon">Siam Paragon</a> and a group of students who were to organise a sandwich activity at the same venue were apprehended and were later placed in detention.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup></p>
<p>Apart from using the three-finger salute and eating sandwiches in public, two other acts of expressing anti-coup stance have been criminalised: reading <a href="/wiki/George_Orwell" title="George Orwell">George Orwell</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Nineteen_Eighty-Four" title="Nineteen Eighty-Four">Nineteen Eighty-Four</a></i> in public, and wearing shirts with certain slogans such as "Peace Please" and "Respect My Vote".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-acsymbol_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-acsymbol-138">[138]</a></sup></p>
<p>Private citizens performing other symbolic acts were also apprehended and detained. These included: an aged woman wearing a mask with the word "People",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> a man shouting "I'm a common citizen who feels shame because there's another coup in my country",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> a fried squid vendor wearing a red shirt,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> a group of people covering their eyes, ears, and mouths,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> a group of movie commentators and artistic activists intending to organise the displaying of the film <i><a href="/wiki/Nineteen_Eighty-Four_(film)" title="Nineteen Eighty-Four (film)">Nineteen Eighty-Four</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup> a group of people holding activities in front of <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Wat_Phra_Si_Mahathat&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Wat Phra Si Mahathat (page does not exist)">Wat Phra Si Mahathat</a>, a temple in Bangkok, to commemorate the transition of absolute monarchy to constitutional monarchy in the <a href="/wiki/Siamese_revolution_of_1932" title="Siamese revolution of 1932">Siamese revolution of 1932</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup> a student playing "<a href="/wiki/La_Marseillaise" title="La Marseillaise">La Marseillaise</a>" in public,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> a group of people holding papers asking "Where are the people?",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sedition_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-sedition-146">[146]</a></sup> and a man holding a paper saying "Holding papers is not a crime".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> All were to be tried in military courts for sedition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sedition_146-1"><a href="#cite_note-sedition-146">[146]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pro-coup">Pro-coup</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2014_Thai_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Pro-coup">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>On 25 May 2014, a pro-coup group held a counter-rally at the Democracy Monument calling on the NCPO to conduct reforms before a general election. The following day, a group of officials from the <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Foreign_Affairs_(Thailand)" title="Ministry of Foreign Affairs (Thailand)">Ministry of Foreign Affairs</a> held a similar rally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_parties">Political parties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2014_Thai_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Political parties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Democrat Party leader <a href="/wiki/Abhisit_Vejjajiva" title="Abhisit Vejjajiva">Abhisit Vejjajiva</a> said on 25 May 2014 that he was sorry that he could not protect democracy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup> The following day, he urged the NCPO to take tough measures against anti-coup groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup></p>
<p>PDRC leader Suthep Thaugsuban alleged that he had talked to General Prayut about how to exclude Thaksin and his family from power as early as 2010. He also claimed that before declaring martial law, General Prayut told him that "Khun Suthep and your masses of PDRC supporters are too exhausted. It's now the duty of the army to take over the task".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-plan_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-plan-151">[151]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Academic_and_religious_sectors">Academic and religious sectors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2014_Thai_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Academic and religious sectors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The coup met with strong reactions from Thai academics, with the majority expressing serious concern over its negative impact on Thailand's democracy and human rights. But some Thai academics argued that there was no other solution to Thailand's problems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup></p>
<p>A group of university lecturers, who call themselves the Assembly for the Defence of Democracy (AFDD), issued a statement on 23 May 2014, emphasizing the right of the people to offer resistance to an illegitimate ruler and requesting the military to promptly release the detained protesters. In its statement, the AFDD said,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote>
<p>A good ruler shall not rule by force. He must be accepted by the public, must gain public consent, must keep his words, and must acquire faith and trust from everyone. If the NCPO treats the people as its enemy and keeps on suppressing them by force, it will face endless resistance and will have to suppress countless people. Eventually, there will be no one left to be ruled.</p>
</blockquote>
<p>On that day, AFDD members gathered in front of the <a href="/wiki/Thammasat_University#Tha_Phra_Chan" title="Thammasat University">Dome Building</a> of Thammasat University to express their anti-coup stance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup> The scholars from the <a href="/wiki/Midnight_University" title="Midnight University">Midnight University</a> also issued a statement denouncing the coup and demanding the resignation of the National Human Rights Commission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup></p>
<p>The academic sector also called on NCPO members to <a href="/wiki/Conflict_of_interest#Disclosure" title="Conflict of interest">disclose their financial interests</a> to the public. However, the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=National_Anti-Corruption_Commission&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="National Anti-Corruption Commission (page does not exist)">National Anti-Corruption Commission</a> said that it is not required by any law.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 26 May 2014, <a href="/wiki/Phra_Paisal_Visalo" title="Phra Paisal Visalo">Phra Paisal Visalo</a>, a monk renowned for his roles in human rights protection, denounced the coup, saying "Even though this coup brought an end to some urgent problems..., it is the beginning of new various problems which are surely more serious".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup></p>
<p>In response to heavy opposition from the academic sector, military officers were stationed at some higher education institutions to prevent academics from expressing political opinions or holding political activities. These included Thammasat University's <a href="/wiki/Thammasat_University#Tha_Phra_Chan" title="Thammasat University">Tha Pra Chan Campus</a> in Bangkok.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup></p>
<p>The NCPO summoned leading pro-democracy scholars. Despite the NCPO threatening that those failing to show up would face criminal penalties, the summoned said they would not submit to the military.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup> A number of scholars who failed to appear were later apprehended, such as Thammasat University law lecturer Sawatree Suksri who was apprehended at <a href="/wiki/Suvarnabhumi_Airport" title="Suvarnabhumi Airport">Suvarnabhumi Airport</a> on 7 June 2014 after travelling back from the United States on a US embassy study trip about human rights.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup></p>
<p>The military also ordered Thai diplomats to take measures to force the return of exiled academicians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-exile_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-exile-162">[162]</a></sup> One of the targets was Pavin Chachavalpongpun, a Thai associate professor at <a href="/wiki/Kyoto_University" title="Kyoto University">Kyoto University</a> who challenged the summons by asking if he could send his pet <a href="/wiki/Chihuahua_(dog)" title="Chihuahua (dog)">chihuahua</a> to meet with General Prayut in his stead.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup> The Thai ambassador in Tokyo and consul general in Osaka have both been told by the military that if they fail to force Pavin to return, they will either be transferred or forced out of the foreign service.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-exile_162-1"><a href="#cite_note-exile-162">[162]</a></sup> On 13 June 2014, the NCPO issued an arrest warrant against Pavin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="International_responses">International responses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2014_Thai_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: International responses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Government_sector">Government sector</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2014_Thai_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Government sector">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Supranational">Supranational</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2014_Thai_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Supranational">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul>
<li><span class="flagicon"><img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="540" data-file-width="810" height="15" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Flag_of_Europe.svg/23px-Flag_of_Europe.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Flag_of_Europe.svg/35px-Flag_of_Europe.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Flag_of_Europe.svg/45px-Flag_of_Europe.svg.png 2x" width="23"/> </span><a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">EU</a>
<ul>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Council_of_the_European_Union" title="Council of the European Union">Council of the European Union</a> concluded on 23 June 2014 that the EU and its member states will not sign the partnership and cooperation agreement with Thailand, until a democratically elected government is in place, and that other agreements will also be affected as appropriate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/European_External_Action_Service" title="European External Action Service">European External Action Service</a> called for the military to accept and respect the constitutional authority of the civilian power and stressed "the importance of holding credible and inclusive elections as soon as feasible".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
</li>
<li><span class="flagicon"><img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="1200" height="15" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Flag_of_the_United_Nations.svg/23px-Flag_of_the_United_Nations.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Flag_of_the_United_Nations.svg/35px-Flag_of_the_United_Nations.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Flag_of_the_United_Nations.svg/45px-Flag_of_the_United_Nations.svg.png 2x" width="23"/> </span><a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">UN</a>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ban_Ki-moon" title="Ban Ki-moon">Ban Ki-moon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Secretary-General_of_the_United_Nations" title="Secretary-General of the United Nations">Secretary-General of the United Nations</a>, issued a statement through his spokesman, expressing concern over the coup, calling for "a prompt return to constitutional, civilian, democratic rule" and movement towards cooperation between the parties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Navi_Pillay" title="Navi Pillay">Navi Pillay</a>, <a href="/wiki/Office_of_the_United_Nations_High_Commissioner_for_Human_Rights" title="Office of the United Nations High Commissioner for Human Rights">United Nations High Commissioner for Human Rights</a>, condemned the coup. She said her office has monitored the situations for the past five months and she is "deeply concerned about the forcible replacement of an elected government, the imposition of martial law, the suspension of the constitution and the emergency measures that are restricting the enjoyment of human rights". She also urged prompt restoration of rule of law in the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
</li>
</ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Countries">Countries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2014_Thai_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Countries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul>
<li><span class="flagicon"><img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="640" data-file-width="1280" height="12" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b9/Flag_of_Australia.svg/23px-Flag_of_Australia.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b9/Flag_of_Australia.svg/35px-Flag_of_Australia.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b9/Flag_of_Australia.svg/46px-Flag_of_Australia.svg.png 2x" width="23"/> </span><a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a> – Australian Foreign Minister <a href="/wiki/Julie_Bishop" title="Julie Bishop">Julie Bishop</a> expressed that she was "gravely concerned" over the military coup and described the situation as "volatile". She also urged Australian travellers to exercise caution and pay close attention to their security, there are an estimated 5000+ Australians currently in Thailand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup></li>
<li><span class="flagicon"><img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="625" height="15" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Flag_of_Cambodia.svg/23px-Flag_of_Cambodia.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Flag_of_Cambodia.svg/35px-Flag_of_Cambodia.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Flag_of_Cambodia.svg/46px-Flag_of_Cambodia.svg.png 2x" width="23"/> </span><a href="/wiki/Cambodia" title="Cambodia">Cambodia</a> – Cambodian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Government_of_Cambodia" title="Government of Cambodia">government officials</a> expressed concerns that tensions could rise at the Cambodia–Thailand border, where a <a href="/wiki/Cambodian%E2%80%93Thai_border_dispute" title="Cambodian–Thai border dispute">dispute</a> has been ongoing since 2008. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Council_of_Ministers_(Cambodia)" title="Council of Ministers (Cambodia)">Council of Ministers</a> spokesman Phay Siphan said "We wish to see this [coup] not jeopardizing democratic transition, maintaining peace and stability, and still respecting [and safeguarding] the will and interest of the Thai people" while adding that nothing is expected to change at the Cambodia–Thailand border. Siphan also said that the government would always respect the mutual interests of the two countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup></li>
<li><span class="flagicon"><img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="1000" height="12" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/cf/Flag_of_Canada.svg/23px-Flag_of_Canada.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/cf/Flag_of_Canada.svg/35px-Flag_of_Canada.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/cf/Flag_of_Canada.svg/46px-Flag_of_Canada.svg.png 2x" width="23"/> </span><a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a> – Foreign Affairs Minister <a href="/wiki/John_Baird_(Canadian_politician)" title="John Baird (Canadian politician)">John Baird</a> condemned the coup and said "This decision violates Thailand's democratic principles and stands in stark contrast to the Army's earlier assurances that its role would be limited to securing public order. We hope and expect the Thai military will return Thailand to civilian rule as soon as possible, respect democratic processes and the rule of law, ensure freedom of expression and assembly, and guarantee due process for those who have been detained."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup></li>
<li><span class="flagicon"><img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="1500" height="15" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Flag_of_Chile.svg/23px-Flag_of_Chile.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Flag_of_Chile.svg/35px-Flag_of_Chile.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Flag_of_Chile.svg/45px-Flag_of_Chile.svg.png 2x" width="23"/> </span><a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">Chile</a> – The Foreign Affairs Ministry issued a statement condemning the coup and saying that they "trust that the political crisis affecting the partner and friendly nation and its people, can be quickly and peacefully resolved through means that will allow the recovery of democratic coexistence".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup></li>
<li><span class="flagicon"><img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="900" height="15" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Flag_of_the_People%27s_Republic_of_China.svg/23px-Flag_of_the_People%27s_Republic_of_China.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Flag_of_the_People%27s_Republic_of_China.svg/35px-Flag_of_the_People%27s_Republic_of_China.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Flag_of_the_People%27s_Republic_of_China.svg/45px-Flag_of_the_People%27s_Republic_of_China.svg.png 2x" width="23"/> </span><a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> – Foreign Ministry issued a brief statement expressing its concern over the situation and hoped for the order to be restored in Thailand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup></li>
<li><span class="flagicon"><img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="450" height="15" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Flag_of_Colombia.svg/23px-Flag_of_Colombia.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Flag_of_Colombia.svg/35px-Flag_of_Colombia.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Flag_of_Colombia.svg/45px-Flag_of_Colombia.svg.png 2x" width="23"/> </span><a href="/wiki/Colombia" title="Colombia">Colombia</a> – The Foreign Affairs Ministry, through a press release, reiterated its concern about the current situation in the "allied nation" of Thailand and condemned the "break of the institutional order that was caused by the coup". Colombia called for dialogue, between the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Thai_Armed_Forces" title="Royal Thai Armed Forces">public armed forces</a> and the Kingdom of Thailand, in order to reestablish a participative Democracy while also "advocating for the constitutional rights of all Thai citizens."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup></li>
<li><span class="flagicon"><img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="900" height="15" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c3/Flag_of_France.svg/23px-Flag_of_France.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c3/Flag_of_France.svg/35px-Flag_of_France.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c3/Flag_of_France.svg/45px-Flag_of_France.svg.png 2x" width="23"/> </span><a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a> – President <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Hollande" title="François Hollande">François Hollande</a> condemned the coup and called for "an immediate return to the constitutional order and for a vote to be organised".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup></li>
